Intelligence</div><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-R6PoUb"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-EehZO nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><h1 class="Qwf2Db-MnozTc An Overview of UNC2891</div></h1></div></div><div class="nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe dEogG">March 16, 2022</div></div></section><div class="EKklye"><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf ZWw7T"><div class="npzWPc"><div class="dzoHJ"><div class="nRhiJb-DX2B6 nRhiJb-DX2B6-OWXEXe-h30Snd"><div class="nRhiJb-j5y3u"><ul class="nRhiJb-Qijihe phRaUe" role="list"><li class="hpHPGf"><a class="nRhiJb-ARYxNe" href="https://x.com/intent/tweet?text=Have%20Your%20Cake%20and%20Eat%20it%20Too?%20An%20Overview%20of%20UNC2891%20@googlecloud&amp;url=https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc2891-overview" track-name="x"track-type="social share"track-metadata-eventdetail="x.com/intent/tweet?text=Have Your Cake and Eat it Too? An Overview of UNC2891&amp;u=cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc2891-overview"track-metadata-module="social icons" target="_blank" rel="noopener"><svg class="nRhiJb-Bz112c nRhiJb-Bz112c-OWXEXe-DX2B6 nRhiJb-Bz112c-OWXEXe-nSuQf" viewBox="0 0 24 24" role="presentation" aria-hidden="true" role="presentation" aria-hidden="true"><path d="M20 2H4c-1.1 0-1.99.9-1.99 2L2 20c0 1.1.9 2 2 2h16c1.1 0 2-.9 2-2V4c0-1.1-.9-2-2-2zm-1 2v3h-2c-.55 0-1 .45-1 1v2h3v3h-3v7h-3v-7h-2v-3h2V7.5C13 5.57 14.57 4 16.5 4H19z"></path></svg></a></li><li class="hpHPGf"><a class="nRhiJb-ARYxNe" href="mailto:?subject=Have%20Your%20Cake%20and%20Eat%20it%20Too?%20An%20Overview%20of%20UNC2891&amp;body=Check%20out%20this%20article%20on%20the%20Cloud%20Blog:%0A%0AHave%20Your%20Cake%20and%20Eat%20it%20Too?%20An%20Overview%20of%20UNC2891%0A%0A%0A%0Ahttps://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc2891-overview" track-name="email"track-type="social share"track-metadata-eventdetail="mailto:?subject=Have Your Cake and Eat it Too? An Overview of UNC2891&amp;body=Check%20out%20this%20article%20on%20the%20Cloud%20Blog:%0A%0AHave Your Cake and Eat it Too? An Overview of UNC2891%0A%0A%0A%0Acloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc2891-overview"track-metadata-module="social icons" target="_blank" rel="noopener"><svg class="nRhiJb-Bz112c nRhiJb-Bz112c-OWXEXe-DX2B6 nRhiJb-Bz112c-OWXEXe-nSuQf" viewBox="0 0 24 24" role="presentation" aria-hidden="true" role="presentation" aria-hidden="true"><path d="M20 4H4c-1.1 0-2 .9-2 2v12c0 1.1.9 2 2 2h16c1.1 0 2-.9 2-2V6c0-1.1-.9-2-2-2zm-.8 2L12 10.8 4.8 6h14.4zM4 18V7.87l8 5.33 8-5.33V18H4z"></path></svg></a></li></ul></div></div></div></div></div><div><section class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-cGMI2b"><section class="DA9Qj nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-fmcmS-oXtfBe"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-c5RTEf"></div><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-qWD73c"><h5 class="cHE8Ub Qwf2Db-MnozTc Qwf2Db-MnozTc-OWXEXe-MnozTc-qWD73c">Mandiant </h5><p class="nRhiJb-qJTHM khCp7b"></p></div></section></div></section><div class="nRhiJb-DARUcf"><div class="nRhiJb-ObfsIf nRhiJb-DbgRPb-wNfPc-ma6Yeb nRhiJb-DbgRPb-qWD73c-cGMI2b"><div class="nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-ibL1re dzoHJ"></div><div class="OYL9D nRhiJb-kR0ZEf-OWXEXe-GV1x9e-OiUrBf" jsname="tx2NYc"><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Written by: Mathew Potaczek, Takahiro Sugiyama, Logeswaran Nadarajan, Yu Nakamura, Josh Homan, Martin Co, Sylvain Hirsch</p>
<hr></span></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>The Mandiant Advanced Practices team previously published a threat research blog post that provided an overview of UNC1945 operations where the actor compromised managed services providers to gain access to targets in the financial and professional consulting industries.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">Since that time, Mandiant has investigated and attributed several intrusions to a threat cluster we believe has a nexus to this actor, currently being tracked as UNC2891. Through these investigations, Mandiant has discovered additional techniques, malware, and utilities being used by UNC2891 alongside those previously observed in use by UNC1945. Despite having identified significant overlaps between these threat clusters, Mandiant has not determined they are attributable to the same actor.</span></p>
<ul>
<li>UNC2891 intrusions appear to be financially motivated and in some cases spanned several years through which the actor had remained largely undetected.</li>
<li>UNC2891 demonstrated fluency and expertise in Unix and Linux environments, mostly through the targeting of Oracle Solaris based systems with TINYSHELL and SLAPSTICK backdoors.</li>
<li>Mandiant observed UNC2891 operate with a high degree of OPSEC and leverage both public and private malware, utilities, and scripts to remove evidence and hinder response efforts.</li>
<li>Mandiant discovered a previously unknown rootkit for Oracle Solaris systems that UNC2891 used to remain hidden in victim networks, we have named this CAKETAP.</li>
<li>One Variant of CAKETAP manipulated messages transiting a victims Automatic Teller Machine (ATM) switching network. It is believed this was leveraged as part of a larger operation to perform unauthorized cash withdrawals at several banks using fraudulent bank cards.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Extensive Use of SLAPSTICK and TINYSHELL Backdoors</h2>
<p>Like past UNC1945 intrusions, Mandiant observed UNC2891 make extensive use of the Pluggable Authentication Module (PAM) based backdoor we track as SLAPSTICK to aid with credential harvesting, and to provide backdoor access to compromised machines in victim networks. As detailed in our previous <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/live-off-the-land-an-overview-of-unc1945" rel="noopener" target="_blank">blog post</a><u>,</u> SLAPSTICK provides persistent backdoor access to infected systems with a hard-coded <em>magical password</em>, it also logs authentication attempts and corresponding passwords in an encrypted log file. Although this is expected to have tremendously assisted UNC2891 with credential harvesting and lateral movement activities, it also provided valuable information to Mandiant Incident Responders. Although SLAPSTICK log files were often timestomped, Mandiant was able to decode them and trace some of the actor’s lateral movement activities through the usage of the backdoor provided <em>magical password</em>.</p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 1: Example SLAPSTICK decoded log (fabricated)</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p><span lang="EN-AU">Alongside SLAPSTICK, UNC2891 often installed a custom variant of the </span><a href="https://github.com/creaktive/tsh" rel="noopener" target="_blank"><span lang="EN-AU">publicly available</span></a><span lang="EN-AU"> TINYSHELL backdoor. UNC2891 TINYSHELL backdoors leveraged an external encrypted configuration file and some variants included additional functionality, such as the ability to communicate via a HTTP proxy with basic authentication. In line with the group’s familiarity with Unix and Linux based systems, UNC2891 often named and configured their TINYSHELL backdoors with values that masqueraded as legitimate services that might be overlooked by investigators, such as systemd (SYSTEMD), name service cache daemon (NCSD), and the Linux at daemon (ATD).</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

</div>
</div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center"><span style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 1: Observed TINYSHELL file paths</span></div>

<p><span lang="EN-AU">In the case of the systemd variant, UNC2891 also leveraged systemd service unit files for persistence of the TINYSHELL backdoor.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">

</tr>
<tr>

<p>[Unit]</p>
<p>Description=Rebuild Hardware Database</p>
<p> </p>

<p>ExecStart=/lib/systemd/systemd-helper</p>

</td>
</tr>
</tbody>

</div>
</div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 3: Service unit file used for TINYSHELL persistence</div>
</div>
<p>Based on analyzed configurations, UNC2891 had configured TINYSHELL backdoors in a multi-hop structure that leveraged several compromised internal servers for command and control. In one case, Mandiant found evidence that suggests the actor had chained different TINYSHELL variants together to obtain remote access to a server inside a network segment with network restrictions.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">To keep their network of TINYSHELL connections hidden, UNC2891 had installed and configured a rootkit to filter out these connections from network connection related APIs (keep reading for details on the CAKETAP rootkit). UNC2891 configured remotely accessible systems with TINYSHELL backdoors that used dynamic DNS domains for their external command and control channel. These domains were created per-host and were not used more than once, the subdomains sometimes resembled the hostname of the compromised machine. Mandiant was unable to collect passive DNS data for these dynamic DNS domains, suggesting that UNC2891 had likely enabled IP resolution for short periods of time when access to the network was required. At one victim, these TINYSHELL backdoors were configured to perform communications using TCP over port 53 and 443, likely as a mechanism to bypass outbound network protections, blend in with existing traffic, and evade detection.</span></p></span></section><section class="QzPuud"><div><section><figure class="NEBdNd"><section class="PBkdHd DhGbH" jscontroller="SCGBie" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><section class="glue-modal glue-modal--dark QHdDac" role="dialog" aria-modal="true"><img class="JcsBte mZzdH ZOnyjc" src="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png" alt="https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png" jsname='P3Vluc' jsaction="click:HTIlC" loading="lazy"/><button class="glue-modal__close-btn" tabindex="0" aria-label="Close this modal"></button></section></section></figure><div class="nRhiJb-cHYyed nRhiJb-DbgRPb-R6PoUb-ma6Yeb ZpqjUe"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><p>Figure 2: Example of TINYSHELL command and control used by UNC2891</p></span></div></section></div></section><section class="Wy08Ac nRhiJb-qJTHM-OWXEXe-hJDwNd nRhiJb-DbgRPb-II5mzb-cGMI2b"><span class="dQQu7c" jsaction="rcuQ6b:npT2md" jscontroller="YSybTb" data-track-type="" soy-skip ssk='5:kbe95'><h2>STEELHOUND, STEELCORGI and Environment Variable Keying</h2>
<p>UNC2891 often made use of the STEELCORGI in-memory dropper which decrypts its embedded payloads by deriving a ChaCha20 key from the value of an environment variable obtained at runtime. In many cases, Mandiant was unable to recover the requisite environment variables to decrypt the embedded payloads. However, in the limited samples we were able to decrypt, UNC2891 had deployed different versions of an extensive toolkit which appears to be developed under the name SUN4ME. SUN4ME contains tools for network reconnaissance, host enumeration, exploitation of known vulnerabilities, log wiping, file operations, as well as common shell utilities. Yoroi has previously <a href="https://yoroi.company/research/opening-steelcorgi-a-sophisticated-apt-swiss-army-knife/" rel="noopener" target="_blank">published</a> information about this toolkit following our previous blog post on <a href="https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/live-off-the-land-an-overview-of-unc1945" rel="noopener" target="_blank">UNC1945’s usage of STEELCORGI</a>.</p>
<p>Mandiant discovered UNC2891 leveraging a similar in-memory dropper that also used environment variables to decrypt its embedded payload but instead relied on RC4 encryption, we have named this STEELHOUND. In addition to functioning as dropper for an embedded payload, STEELHOUND is also able to encrypt new payloads by encrypting a target binary and writing it to disk along with a copy of itself and an end-of-file configuration.</p>
<h2>WINGHOOK and WINGCRACK</h2>
<p>During these investigations, Mandiant also discovered a family of keylogger malware we have named WINGHOOK and WINGCRACK.</p>
<ul>
<li>WINGHOOK is a keylogger for Linux and Unix based operating systems. It is packaged as a shared library (SO file) that hooks the <em>read</em> and <em>fgets </em>functions, which are two common functions used for processing user input. The captured data is stored in an encoded format in the directory <em>/var/tmp/ </em>with a filename that begins with <em>.zmanDw</em>.</li>
<li>WINGCRACK is a utility that can decode and display the content of files containing encoded keylog data from WINGHOOK. The malware author appears to refer to these encoded files as “schwing” files.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Utilities Observed</h2>
<p>Mandiant previously observed UNC1945 use a large amount of different public and private tools during their intrusions, and this was also true for UNC2891. Mandiant discovered additional utilities that were leveraged by UNC2891:</p>
<ul>
<li>BINBASH is a simple ELF utility that executes a shell after setting the group ID and user ID to either "root" or specified values. BINBASH appears to be a <a href="https://packetstormsecurity.com/files/23336/Slx2k001.txt.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank">compilation of the source code</a>.</li>
<li>WIPERIGHT is an ELF utility that clears specific log entries on Linux and Unix based systems. It can remove entries associated with a given user in the lastlog, utmp/utmpx, wtmp/wtmpx, and pacct logs. It appears to have originated from <a href="http://www.afn.org/~afn28925/wipe.c" rel="noopener" target="_blank">available source code</a>, and possibly a <a href="https://packetstormsecurity.com/files/23336/Slx2k001.txt.html" rel="noopener" target="_blank">more recent version</a>.</li>
<li>MIGLOGCLEANER is another ELF utility that wipes logs or remove certain strings from logs on Linux and Unix based systems. It is <a href="https://github.com/Kabot/mig-logcleaner-resurrected" rel="noopener" target="_blank">publicly available on GitHub</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Whilst seemingly uncommon amongst threat actors, UNC2891 frequently used the uuencoding scheme to encode and decode files, such as malware binaries or files containing output from extensive host enumeration scripts. The actor often leveraged simple Perl wrapper scripts that performed uuencoding and uudecoding functions.</p>
<h2>CAKETAP</h2>
<p>CAKETAP is a kernel module rootkit that UNC2891 deployed on key server infrastructure running Oracle Solaris. CAKETAP can hide network connections, processes, and files. During initialization, it removes itself from the loaded modules list and updates the <em>last_module_id</em> with the previously loaded module to hide its presence.</p>
<p>A hook is installed into the function <em>ipcl_get_next_conn</em>, as well as several functions in the <em>ip</em> module. This enables CAKETAP to filter out any connections that match an actor-configured IP address or port (local or remote).</p>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">One way to identify CAKETAP running on a Solaris system is to check for the presence of this hook. The following shows an example command to identify a hooked </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">ipcl_get_next_conn</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> function (Note: The </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">mdb</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> command may require special permissions on the system):</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>root@solaris:~# echo 'ipcl_get_next_conn::dis -n 0 ; ::quit' | mdb -k</code></pre>
<p>The output in a clean SPARC Solaris system would look similar to the following:</p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%"> </div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<pre><code>ipcl_get_next_conn: save %sp, -0xb0, %sp</code></pre>
</div>
<p>A hooked function would begin with the <em>sethi</em> instruction as follows (the constant <em>0x11971c00</em> will change from instance to instance depending on where CAKETAP is loaded):</p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>ipcl_get_next_conn: sethi %hi(0x11971c00), %g1</code></pre>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">Additional hooks are installed into the </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">mkdirat</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> (make directory at) and </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">getdents64</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> (get directory entries) system calls. CAKETAP uses the </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">mkdirat</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> hook to receive commands from paths containing the signal string. Commands include configuring network filters, display or update its configuration, and to unhide itself. The </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">getdents64</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> hook enables CAKETAP to hide files or directories on the file system containing the secret signal string. Table 4 contains the signal strings for the CAKETAP hooks.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%"><table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Secret</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Usage</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">.caahGss187</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">mkdirat hook signal string</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">.zaahGss187</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">getdents64 hook signal string</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%">Table 4: Observed secrets for CAKETAP hooks</div>
</div>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">The </span><em><span lang="EN-AU">mkdirat</span></em><span lang="EN-AU"> hook enabled UNC2891 to control and configure CAKETAP through existing backdoor access to compromised servers by issuing shell commands that leverage these system calls (e.g. mkdir for mkdirat). A single character appended to the signal string indicated which command was to be executed. The following commands were observed:</span></p>
<p> </p>
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%">
<div style="color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%"><table>
<tbody>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Command</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Function</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Empty</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Add the CAKETAP module back to loaded modules list</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">M</td>

</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">I</td>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Add a network filter (format p)</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">i</td>

</tr>

</tr>
<tr>
<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">p</td>

</tr>
<tr>

<td style="border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px">Displays the current configuration</td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table></div>
</div>
</div>
<div style="color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%;text-align:center">Table 5: Observed CAKETAP commands</div>
</div>
<p>For example, to configure a new network filter and display the current configuration, the following commands might be used:</p>
<ul>
<li><em>mkdir /some/path/.caahGss187I192.168.1.10p80 - </em>Add network filter for 192.168.1.10:80</li>
<li><em>mkdir /some/path/.caahGss187S </em>- Display current configuration</li>
</ul>
<p>The hook installed into <em>getdents64</em> filtered output to hide presence of the signal string in directory contents.</p>
<p>Mandiant observed UNC2891 load CAKETAP with the module name <em>ipstat</em> from attacker created directories that often resided somewhere inside the <em>/var</em> directory tree.</p>
<h2>CAKETAP Unauthorized Transactions</h2>
<p>Memory forensics from one victim’s ATM switch server revealed a variant of CAKETAP with additional network hooking functionality that intercepted specific messages relating to card and pin verification. Evidence suggests that this variant of CAKETAP was used as part of an operation to perform unauthorized transactions using fraudulent bank cards.</p>
<p>This CAKETAP variant targeted specific messages destined for the Payment Hardware Security Module (HSM). This additional network hooking performed several functions:</p>
<ol>
<li>Manipulation of card verification messages:<br>CAKETAP altered the mode of certain outgoing messages to disable card verification. This resulted in the HSM not performing the proper card verification and instead generating a valid response. Fraudulent bank cards generated verification messages using a custom algorithm using the Primary Account Number (PAN) and other parameters which served as a “marker” for CAKETAP. CAKETAP examined outgoing messages and if it matched the algorithm, CAKETAP identified the card as fraudulent and stored the PAN in memory to use in the following step.</li>
<li>Replay of PIN verification messages:<br>CAKETAP examined outgoing PIN verification messages that matched certain conditions and identified those with a Primary Account Number (PAN) that reflected a fraudulent card. If the message was not for a fraudulent card, it would save the message internally and send it unmodified, as to not interrupt legitimate ATM PIN verifications. However, if it was for a fraudulent card, CAKETAP would instead replace the message content with data from a previously saved message. This was effectively a replay attack that resulted in a bypass of PIN verification for fraudulent cards.</li>
</ol>
<p>Based on Mandiant’s investigation findings, we believe that CAKETAP was leveraged by UNC2891 as part of a larger operation to successfully use fraudulent bank cards to perform unauthorized cash withdrawals from ATM terminals at several banks.</p>
<h2>Conclusion</h2>
<p>UNC2891 maintains a high level of OPSEC and employs several techniques to evade detection. The actor uses their skill and experience to take full advantage of the decreased visibility and security measures that are often present in Unix and Linux environments. Mandiant expects that UNC2891 will continue to capitalize on this and perform similar operations for financial gain that target mission critical systems running these operating systems.</p>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">While some of the overlaps between UNC2891 and UNC1945 are notable, it is not conclusive enough to attribute the intrusions to a single threat group. For example, it is possible that significant portions of UNC2891 and UNC1945 activity are carried out by an entity that is a common resource to multiple threat actors, which could explain the perceived difference in intrusion objectives—a common malware developer or an intrusion partner, for example. Regardless, Mandiant is releasing this information on the actor to raise awareness of the fraudulent activity and aid defenders in uncovering further UNC2891 operations.</span></p>
<h2>YARA</h2>
<p><span lang="EN-AU">The following YARA rules are not intended to be used on production systems or to inform blocking rules without first being validated through an organization's own internal testing processes to ensure appropriate performance and limit the risk of false positives. These rules are intended to serve as a starting point for hunting efforts to identify samples, however, they may need adjustment over time if the malware family changes.</span></p>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule TINYSHELL
{
meta:
author = "Mandiant "
strings:
$sb1 = { C6 00 48 C6 4? ?? 49 C6 4? ?? 49 C6 4? ?? 4C C6 4? ?? 53 C6 4? ?? 45 C6 4? ?? 54 C6 4? ?? 3D C6 4? ?? 46 C6 4? ?? 00 }
$sb2 = { C6 00 54 C6 4? ?? 4D C6 4? ?? 45 C6 4? ?? 3D C6 4? ?? 52 }
$ss1 = "fork" ascii fullword wide
$ss2 = "socket" ascii fullword wide
$ss3 = "bind" ascii fullword wide
$ss4 = "listen" ascii fullword wide
$ss5 = "accept" ascii fullword wide
$ss6 = "alarm" ascii fullword wide
$ss7 = "shutdown" ascii fullword wide
$ss8 = "creat" ascii fullword wide
$ss9 = "write" ascii fullword wide
$ss10 = "open" ascii fullword wide
$ss11 = "read" ascii fullword wide
$ss12 = "execl" ascii fullword wide
$ss13 = "gethostbyname" ascii fullword wide
$ss14 = "connect" ascii fullword wide
condition:
uint32(0) == 0x464c457f and 1 of ($sb*) and 10 of ($ss*)
}</code></pre>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule TINYSHELL_SPARC
{
meta:
author = &quot;Mandiant&quot;
strings:
$sb_xor_1 = { DA 0A 80 0C 82 18 40 0D C2 2A 00 0B 96 02 E0 01 98 03 20 01 82 1B 20 04 80 A0 00 01 82 60 20 00 98 0B 00 01 C2 4A 00 0B 80 A0 60 00 32 BF FF F5 C2 0A 00 0B 81 C3 E0 08 }
$sb_xor_2 = { C6 4A 00 00 80 A0 E0 00 02 40 00 0B C8 0A 00 00 85 38 60 00 C4 09 40 02 84 18 80 04 C4 2A 00 00 82 00 60 01 80 A0 60 04 83 64 60 00 10 6F FF F5 90 02 20 01 81 C3 E0 08 }
condition:
uint32(0) == 0x464C457F and (uint16(0x10) &amp; 0x0200 == 0x0200) and (uint16(0x12) &amp; 0x0200 == 0x0200) and 1 of them
}</code></pre>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule SLAPSTICK
{
meta:
author = "Mandiant "
strings:
$ss1 = "%Y %b %d %H:%M:%S \x00"
$ss2 = "%-23s %-23s %-23s\x00"
$ss3 = "%-23s %-23s %-23s %-23s %-23s %s\x0a\x00"
condition:
(uint32(0) == 0x464c457f) and all of them
}</code></pre>
<p> </p>
<pre><code>rule STEELCORGI
{
    meta:
        author = "Mandiant "
    strings:
        $s1 = "\x00\xff/\xffp\xffr\xffo\xffc\xff/\xffs\xffe\xffl\xfff\xff/\xffe\xffx\xffe\x00"
        $s2 = "\x00\xff/\xffv\xffa\xffr\xff/\xffl\xffi\xffb\xff/\xffd\xffb\xffu\xffs\xff/\xffm\xffa\xffc\xffh\xffi\xffn\xffe\xff-\xffi\xffd\x00"
        $sb1 = { FE 1B 7A DE 23 D1 E9 A1 1D 7F 9E C1 FD A4 }
        $sb2 = { 3B 8D 4F 45 7C 4F 6A 6C D8 2F 1F B2 19 C4 45 6A 6A 
    condition:
        (uint32(0) == 0x464c457f) and all of them
}</code></pre>

<h2 style="text-align:start">MITRE ATT&amp;CK</h2>
<ul style="text-align:start">
<li>Discovery:
<ul>
<li>T1016:System Network Configuration Discovery</li>
<li>T1018:Remote System Discovery</li>
<li>T1049:System Network Connections Discovery</li>
<li>T1082:System Information Discovery</li>
<li>T1083:File and Directory Discovery</li>
<li>T1135:Network Share Discovery</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Lateral Movement:
<ul>
<li>T1021:Remote Services</li>
<li>T1021.004:SSH</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Credential Access:
<ul>
<li>T1003:OS Credential Dumping</li>
<li>T1003.008:/etc/passwd and /etc/shadow</li>
<li>T1110:Brute Force</li>
<li>T1110.001:Password Guessing</li>
<li>T1552:Unsecured Credentials</li>
<li>T1552.003:Bash History</li>
<li>T1552.004:Private Keys</li>
<li>T1556.003:Pluggable Authentication Modules</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Command and Control:
<ul>
<li>T1090:Proxy</li>
<li>T1095:Non-Application Layer Protocol</li>
<li>T1105:Ingress Tool Transfer</li>
<li>T1572:Protocol Tunneling</li>
<li>T1573.001:Symmetric Cryptography</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Execution:
<ul>
<li>T1053.001:At (Linux)</li>

<li>T1059.004:Unix Shell</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Collection:
<ul>
<li>T1056.001:Keylogging</li>
<li>T1560:Archive Collected Data</li>

<li>T1560.002:Archive via Library</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Defense Evasion:
<ul>
<li>T1014:Rootkit</li>
<li>T1027:Obfuscated Files or Information</li>

<li>T1070.004:File Deletion</li>
<li>T1070.006:Timestomp</li>

An Overview of UNC2891 | Mandiant\",\"description\":\"\",\"image\":\"https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/03_ThreatIntelligenceWebsiteBannerIdeas_BA.max-2600x2600.png\",\"author\":[{\"@type\":\"Person\",\"name\":\"Mandiant \",\"url\":\"\"}],\"datePublished\":\"2022-03-16\",\"publisher\":{\"@type\":\"Organization\",\"name\":\"Google Cloud\",\"logo\":{\"@type\":\"ImageObject\",\"url\":\"https://www.gstatic.com/devrel-devsite/prod/v8bb8fa0afe9a8c3a776ebeb25d421bb443344d789b3607754dfabea418b8c4be/cloud/images/cloud-logo.svg\"}},\"url\":\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/unc2891-overview\",\"keywords\":[\"Threat Intelligence\",\"Security \\u0026 Identity\"],\"timeRequired\":\"PT15M\"}\u003c/script\u003e"],["Have Your Cake and Eat it Too? An Overview of UNC2891"],null,null,[[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eWritten by: Mathew Potaczek, Takahiro Sugiyama, Logeswaran Nadarajan, Yu Nakamura, Josh Homan, Martin Co, Sylvain Hirsch\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003chr\u003e"]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003eThe Mandiant Advanced Practices team previously published a threat research blog post that provided an overview of UNC1945 operations where the actor compromised managed services providers to gain access to targets in the financial and professional consulting industries.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eSince that time, Mandiant has investigated and attributed several intrusions to a threat cluster we believe has a nexus to this actor, currently being tracked as UNC2891. Through these investigations, Mandiant has discovered additional techniques, malware, and utilities being used by UNC2891 alongside those previously observed in use by UNC1945. Despite having identified significant overlaps between these threat clusters, Mandiant has not determined they are attributable to the same actor.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUNC2891 intrusions appear to be financially motivated and in some cases spanned several years through which the actor had remained largely undetected.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUNC2891 demonstrated fluency and expertise in Unix and Linux environments, mostly through the targeting of Oracle Solaris based systems with TINYSHELL and SLAPSTICK backdoors.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMandiant observed UNC2891 operate with a high degree of OPSEC and leverage both public and private malware, utilities, and scripts to remove evidence and hinder response efforts.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMandiant discovered a previously unknown rootkit for Oracle Solaris systems that UNC2891 used to remain hidden in victim networks, we have named this CAKETAP.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eOne Variant of CAKETAP manipulated messages transiting a victims Automatic Teller Machine (ATM) switching network. It is believed this was leveraged as part of a larger operation to perform unauthorized cash withdrawals at several banks using fraudulent bank cards.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eExtensive Use of SLAPSTICK and TINYSHELL Backdoors\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eLike past UNC1945 intrusions, Mandiant observed UNC2891 make extensive use of the Pluggable Authentication Module (PAM) based backdoor we track as SLAPSTICK to aid with credential harvesting, and to provide backdoor access to compromised machines in victim networks. As detailed in our previous \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/live-off-the-land-an-overview-of-unc1945\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eblog post\u003c/a\u003e\u003cu\u003e,\u003c/u\u003e SLAPSTICK provides persistent backdoor access to infected systems with a hard-coded \u003cem\u003emagical password\u003c/em\u003e, it also logs authentication attempts and corresponding passwords in an encrypted log file. Although this is expected to have tremendously assisted UNC2891 with credential harvesting and lateral movement activities, it also provided valuable information to Mandiant Incident Responders. Although SLAPSTICK log files were often timestomped, Mandiant was able to decode them and trace some of the actor’s lateral movement activities through the usage of the backdoor provided \u003cem\u003emagical password\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 1: Example SLAPSTICK decoded log (fabricated)\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png 800w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png 800w"," 800px,  800px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig1.max-800x800.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eAlongside SLAPSTICK, UNC2891 often installed a custom variant of the \u003c/span\u003e\u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/creaktive/tsh\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003epublicly available\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e TINYSHELL backdoor. UNC2891 TINYSHELL backdoors leveraged an external encrypted configuration file and some variants included additional functionality, such as the ability to communicate via a HTTP proxy with basic authentication. In line with the group’s familiarity with Unix and Linux based systems, UNC2891 often named and configured their TINYSHELL backdoors with values that masqueraded as legitimate services that might be overlooked by investigators, such as systemd (SYSTEMD), name service cache daemon (NCSD), and the Linux at daemon (ATD).\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv persistence\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBased on analyzed configurations, UNC2891 had configured TINYSHELL backdoors in a multi-hop structure that leveraged several compromised internal servers for command and control. In one case, Mandiant found evidence that suggests the actor had chained different TINYSHELL variants together to obtain remote access to a server inside a network segment with network restrictions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eTo keep their network of TINYSHELL connections hidden, UNC2891 had installed and configured a rootkit to filter out these connections from network connection related APIs (keep reading for details on the CAKETAP rootkit). UNC2891 configured remotely accessible systems with TINYSHELL backdoors that used dynamic DNS domains for their external command and control channel. These domains were created per-host and were not used more than once, the subdomains sometimes resembled the hostname of the compromised machine. Mandiant was unable to collect passive DNS data for these dynamic DNS domains, suggesting that UNC2891 had likely enabled IP resolution for short periods of time when access to the network was required. At one victim, these TINYSHELL backdoors were configured to perform communications using TCP over port 53 and 443, likely as a mechanism to bypass outbound network protections, blend in with existing traffic, and evade detection.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e"]]],[null,null,null,null,null,null,null,null,[[[null,"\u003cp\u003eFigure 2: Example of TINYSHELL command and control used by UNC2891\u003c/p\u003e"],["https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1100x1100.png 1060w, https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png 1402w"," 1060px,  1402px","https://storage.googleapis.com/gweb-cloudblog-publish/images/unc2891-fig2_hcpf.max-1500x1500.png"],null,3]]],[null,null,[null,[null,"\u003ch2\u003eSTEELHOUND, STEELCORGI and Environment Variable Keying\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC2891 often made use of the STEELCORGI in-memory dropper which decrypts its embedded payloads by deriving a ChaCha20 key from the value of an environment variable obtained at runtime. In many cases, Mandiant was unable to recover the requisite environment variables to decrypt the embedded payloads. However, in the limited samples we were able to decrypt, UNC2891 had deployed different versions of an extensive toolkit which appears to be developed under the name SUN4ME. SUN4ME contains tools for network reconnaissance, host enumeration, exploitation of known vulnerabilities, log wiping, file operations, as well as common shell utilities. Yoroi has previously \u003ca href\u003d\"https://yoroi.company/research/opening-steelcorgi-a-sophisticated-apt-swiss-army-knife/\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003epublished\u003c/a\u003e information about this toolkit following our previous blog post on \u003ca href\u003d\"https://cloud.google.com/blog/topics/threat-intelligence/live-off-the-land-an-overview-of-unc1945\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eUNC1945’s usage of STEELCORGI\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant discovered UNC2891 leveraging a similar in-memory dropper that also used environment variables to decrypt its embedded payload but instead relied on RC4 encryption, we have named this STEELHOUND. In addition to functioning as dropper for an embedded payload, STEELHOUND is also able to encrypt new payloads by encrypting a target binary and writing it to disk along with a copy of itself and an end-of-file configuration.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eWINGHOOK and WINGCRACK\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eDuring these investigations, Mandiant also discovered a family of keylogger malware we have named WINGHOOK and WINGCRACK.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWINGHOOK is a keylogger for Linux and Unix based operating systems. It is packaged as a shared library (SO file) that hooks the \u003cem\u003eread\u003c/em\u003e and \u003cem\u003efgets \u003c/em\u003efunctions, which are two common functions used for processing user input. The captured data is stored in an encoded format in the directory \u003cem\u003e/var/tmp/ \u003c/em\u003ewith a filename that begins with \u003cem\u003e.zmanDw\u003c/em\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWINGCRACK is a utility that can decode and display the content of files containing encoded keylog data from WINGHOOK. The malware author appears to refer to these encoded files as “schwing” files.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eUtilities Observed\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant previously observed UNC1945 use a large amount of different public and private tools during their intrusions, and this was also true for UNC2891. Mandiant discovered additional utilities that were leveraged by UNC2891:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eBINBASH is a simple ELF utility that executes a shell after setting the group ID and user ID to either \"root\" or specified values. BINBASH appears to be a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://packetstormsecurity.com/files/23336/Slx2k001.txt.html\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003ecompilation of the source code\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eWIPERIGHT is an ELF utility that clears specific log entries on Linux and Unix based systems. It can remove entries associated with a given user in the lastlog, utmp/utmpx, wtmp/wtmpx, and pacct logs. It appears to have originated from \u003ca href\u003d\"http://www.afn.org/~afn28925/wipe.c\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003eavailable source code\u003c/a\u003e, and possibly a \u003ca href\u003d\"https://packetstormsecurity.com/files/23336/Slx2k001.txt.html\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003emore recent version\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eMIGLOGCLEANER is another ELF utility that wipes logs or remove certain strings from logs on Linux and Unix based systems. It is \u003ca href\u003d\"https://github.com/Kabot/mig-logcleaner-resurrected\" rel\u003d\"noopener\" target\u003d\"_blank\"\u003epublicly available on GitHub\u003c/a\u003e.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eWhilst seemingly uncommon amongst threat actors, UNC2891 frequently used the uuencoding scheme to encode and decode files, such as malware binaries or files containing output from extensive host enumeration scripts. The actor often leveraged simple Perl wrapper scripts that performed uuencoding and uudecoding functions.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eCAKETAP\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eCAKETAP is a kernel module rootkit that UNC2891 deployed on key server infrastructure running Oracle Solaris. CAKETAP can hide network connections, processes, and files. During initialization, it removes itself from the loaded modules list and updates the \u003cem\u003elast_module_id\u003c/em\u003e with the previously loaded module to hide its presence.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA hook is installed into the function \u003cem\u003eipcl_get_next_conn\u003c/em\u003e, as well as several functions in the \u003cem\u003eip\u003c/em\u003e module. This enables CAKETAP to filter out any connections that match an actor-configured IP address or port (local or remote).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eOne way to identify CAKETAP running on a Solaris system is to check for the presence of this hook. The following shows an example command to identify a hooked \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eipcl_get_next_conn\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e function (Note: The \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003emdb\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e command may require special permissions on the system):\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eroot@solaris:~# echo 'ipcl_get_next_conn::dis -n 0 ; ::quit' | mdb -k\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe output in a clean SPARC Solaris system would look similar to the following:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e \u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eipcl_get_next_conn: save %sp, -0xb0, %sp\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eA hooked function would begin with the \u003cem\u003esethi\u003c/em\u003e instruction as follows (the constant \u003cem\u003e0x11971c00\u003c/em\u003e will change from instance to instance depending on where CAKETAP is loaded):\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003eipcl_get_next_conn: sethi %hi(0x11971c00), %g1\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eAdditional hooks are installed into the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003emkdirat\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e (make directory at) and \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003egetdents64\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e (get directory entries) system calls. CAKETAP uses the \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003emkdirat\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e hook to receive commands from paths containing the signal string. Commands include configuring network filters, display or update its configuration, and to unhide itself. The \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003egetdents64\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e hook enables CAKETAP to hide files or directories on the file system containing the secret signal string. Table 4 contains the signal strings for the CAKETAP hooks.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\u003ctable\u003e\n\u003ctbody\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eSecret\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003eUsage\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e.caahGss187\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003emkdirat hook signal string\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003ctr\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003e.zaahGss187\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003ctd style\u003d\"border:1px solid #000000;padding:16px\"\u003egetdents64 hook signal string\u003c/td\u003e\n\u003c/tr\u003e\n\u003c/tbody\u003e\n\u003c/table\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;display:block;font-size:16px;font-style:italic;margin-top:8px;width:100%\"\u003eTable 4: Observed secrets for CAKETAP hooks\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eThe \u003c/span\u003e\u003cem\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003emkdirat\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/em\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003e hook enabled UNC2891 to control and configure CAKETAP through existing backdoor access to compromised servers by issuing shell commands that leverage these system calls (e.g. mkdir for mkdirat). A single character appended to the signal string indicated which command was to be executed. The following commands were observed:\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv style\u003d\"color:#5f6368;overflow-x:auto;overflow-y:hidden;width:100%\"\u003e\n\u003cdiv commands\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eFor example, to configure a new network filter and display the current configuration, the following commands might be used:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cem\u003emkdir /some/path/.caahGss187I192.168.1.10p80 - \u003c/em\u003eAdd network filter for 192.168.1.10:80\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003cem\u003emkdir /some/path/.caahGss187S \u003c/em\u003e- Display current configuration\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThe hook installed into \u003cem\u003egetdents64\u003c/em\u003e filtered output to hide presence of the signal string in directory contents.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMandiant observed UNC2891 load CAKETAP with the module name \u003cem\u003eipstat\u003c/em\u003e from attacker created directories that often resided somewhere inside the \u003cem\u003e/var\u003c/em\u003e directory tree.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eCAKETAP Unauthorized Transactions\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eMemory forensics from one victim’s ATM switch server revealed a variant of CAKETAP with additional network hooking functionality that intercepted specific messages relating to card and pin verification. Evidence suggests that this variant of CAKETAP was used as part of an operation to perform unauthorized transactions using fraudulent bank cards.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eThis CAKETAP variant targeted specific messages destined for the Payment Hardware Security Module (HSM). This additional network hooking performed several functions:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eManipulation of card verification messages:\u003cbr\u003eCAKETAP altered the mode of certain outgoing messages to disable card verification. This resulted in the HSM not performing the proper card verification and instead generating a valid response. Fraudulent bank cards generated verification messages using a custom algorithm using the Primary Account Number (PAN) and other parameters which served as a “marker” for CAKETAP. CAKETAP examined outgoing messages and if it matched the algorithm, CAKETAP identified the card as fraudulent and stored the PAN in memory to use in the following step.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eReplay of PIN verification messages:\u003cbr\u003eCAKETAP examined outgoing PIN verification messages that matched certain conditions and identified those with a Primary Account Number (PAN) that reflected a fraudulent card. If the message was not for a fraudulent card, it would save the message internally and send it unmodified, as to not interrupt legitimate ATM PIN verifications. However, if it was for a fraudulent card, CAKETAP would instead replace the message content with data from a previously saved message. This was effectively a replay attack that resulted in a bypass of PIN verification for fraudulent cards.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eBased on Mandiant’s investigation findings, we believe that CAKETAP was leveraged by UNC2891 as part of a larger operation to successfully use fraudulent bank cards to perform unauthorized cash withdrawals from ATM terminals at several banks.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eConclusion\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003eUNC2891 maintains a high level of OPSEC and employs several techniques to evade detection. The actor uses their skill and experience to take full advantage of the decreased visibility and security measures that are often present in Unix and Linux environments. Mandiant expects that UNC2891 will continue to capitalize on this and perform similar operations for financial gain that target mission critical systems running these operating systems.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eWhile some of the overlaps between UNC2891 and UNC1945 are notable, it is not conclusive enough to attribute the intrusions to a single threat group. For example, it is possible that significant portions of UNC2891 and UNC1945 activity are carried out by an entity that is a common resource to multiple threat actors, which could explain the perceived difference in intrusion objectives—a common malware developer or an intrusion partner, for example. Regardless, Mandiant is releasing this information on the actor to raise awareness of the fraudulent activity and aid defenders in uncovering further UNC2891 operations.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003ch2\u003eYARA\u003c/h2\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e\u003cspan lang\u003d\"EN-AU\"\u003eThe following YARA rules are not intended to be used on production systems or to inform blocking rules without first being validated through an organization's own internal testing processes to ensure appropriate performance and limit the risk of false positives. These rules are intended to serve as a starting point for hunting efforts to identify samples, however, they may need adjustment over time if the malware family changes.\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp\u003e \u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cpre\u003e\u003ccode\u003erule TINYSHELL\r\n{\r\nmeta:\r\nauthor \u003d \"Mandiant \"\r\nstrings:\r\n$sb1 \u003d { C6 00 48
